> > Entries may be used for servicing requests even if there are less
> > buffers than the number of entries. Most requests don't use the full
> > payload buffer size, so for example with incremental buffer
>
> I think that is an argument for multi-size bufs per ring.

Which is what the buffer pool provides (decoupling buffers from
entries) if we choose to later add that in the future. With 1:1
entry:buffer mapping, each entry requires a buffer that supports the
max payload size at registration time.

>
> > consumption, this would allow multiple entries to use non-overlapping
> > regions of the same buffer concurrently. As well, if we want to
> > support multiple buffer sizes per ring, there shouldn't be a 1:1
> > mapping between ents and buffers. There's also some requests that
>
> I can counter argue that fuse-server can just send ring entries with a
> different payload sizes - fuse-server could dynamically create as many
> ring entries of the right size as needed.

I don't think this is a good idea. The server would have to
guesstimate this as it doesn't know the request size until it sees the
request. The server could speculatively pre-register ring ents ahead
of time but there's no guarantee that would be the right size for the
ring entry either. And once added, if the workload pattern changes,
then the ent and allocated buffer may not be useful anymore. This also
adds (imo needless) complexity to the kernel side. Additionally, the
dynamic ring ents would in the ideal case have payload buffers pinned,
which there is not an non-messy way to do (for buffer pools, the pool
buffer pinning goes through io-uring registered buffers infrastructure
prior to being passed to fuse at registration time). Imo the better
way if you wanted to dynamically add ents would be to add a new buffer
pool to the ring.

>
> > don't need to use payload buffers at all (eg flush, fsync, rmdir,
>
> Could be handled by multi buf sizes as well.

These requests don't require payload buffers.

This series already handles this case (eg not assigning a buffer from
the pool to the request).

>
> > etc).  As well for registered buffers / buffer pinning, it is more
> > optimal for the buffer pool to be allocated contiguously and pinned
> > contiguously rather than passed in one by one as a 1:1 entry:buffer
> > mapping.
>
> I don't think how the buffer is passed to fuse-client/kernel matters for
> (userspace) allocation. I had written before that libfuse's buffer
> allocation is not ideal, I had it continuous in initial development,
> then run into bug and for simplicity changed to per entry allocation.
> Switching back to larger allocations is simple.
> Regarding page mapping/pinning, why does it matter if the buffer in
> kernel is known as per entry or per allocation (assuming payload buffers
> are page aligned)?

It does matter how the buffers are passed to fuse for page pinning.
Please take a look at patch 8 ([PATCH v3 08/10] fuse: support
registered buffers as buffer pools in io-uring) [1]. The buffer pool
is contiguously allocated and registered as an io-uring registered
buffer, and then passed to fuse and by that point, the buffers have
already been pinned by io-uring.

Even irrespective of page pinning, it would still be good to pass the
buffers contiguously up front as a pool instead of per-ent, as the
purpose of this series is to decouple the 1:1 entry:buffer mapping. Eg
if the request an ent is servicing does not require buffers, a buffer
should not be reserved for that ent, amongst other benefits of a
decoupled mapping.

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260522205823.1597313-9-joannelkoong@gmail.com/

>
> I think the more important part for buf rings are pinned headers. For 4K
> pages the loss is maybe still acceptable and the remaining size could be
> used for small requests and I guess would have a perf benefit. For 64K
> pages the loss is quite a lot.

That is orthogonal to this series. This series focuses on payload
buffers, and header pinning is not part of this series as
bufpools/bufrings do not need pinned headers, as it makes no
difference for performance. As mentioned in the previous message, when
I was working on a separate series for removing the dispatch overhead,
I implemented header pinning as part of that, as that is when header
pinning becomes actually useful.

>
>
> Maybe let's summarize
>
> Bufrings
> - Fixed size, allocated at startup, cannot be easily increased or decreased
> - Allows 0 size payloads (FUSE requests that only need the header)
>
> Entries with payload
> - Allows more flexible setup - fuse server _could_ (not implemented as
> of now) create entries based on incoming IO.
>
> Neither provides real multi-size buffers per ring.
>

I think perhaps there is a misunderstanding of the patches in this series.

This series implements buffer pools, which decouple the 1:1 mapping
between ents and buffers that currently exists. Right now, ents and
buffers are tightly coupled where each entry has its own dedicated
payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where each buffer
must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size.

This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool where the buffers
are not statically mapped to an ent at registration time. This
decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use
fuse-io-uring. It is the necessary stepping stone for future
optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption (allowing
multiple entries to use non-overlapping regions of the same buffer
concurrently) and supporting multi-size buffers per ring.

Thanks,
Joanne

>
>
> Thanks,
> Bernd
>

> >>

> > request. The server could speculatively pre-register ring ents ahead
>
> How expensive (in terms of memory) usage it is to set up a few small
> buffers? Compared to one large buffer?

It's not just about the memory savings. Fundamentally, I believe it's
the wrong abstraction to statically couple ents to buffers 1:1 as a
fixed mapping. Entries and buffers serve different purposes. Entries
handle request flow, buffers hold data. Statically coupling them 1:1
is inflexible, introduces unnecessary complexity, and imo has no added
benefit. This series decouples these two concepts. It allows
dynamically adding ents in the future as *generic* ents not tied to a
particular buffer size (eg the added ent can be used to service *any*
request size, not just the size of the buffer it's tied to). With
tying ents to buffers, the server is stuck with a static distribution
that can't adapt to workload changes.

>
> > of time but there's no guarantee that would be the right size for the
> > ring entry either. And once added, if the workload pattern changes,
> > then the ent and allocated buffer may not be useful anymore. This also
> > adds (imo needless) complexity to the kernel side. Additionally, the
> > dynamic ring ents would in the ideal case have payload buffers pinned,
> > which there is not an non-messy way to do (for buffer pools, the pool
> > buffer pinning goes through io-uring registered buffers infrastructure
> > prior to being passed to fuse at registration time). Imo the better
> > way if you wanted to dynamically add ents would be to add a new buffer
> > pool to the ring.
>
> I think the whole point here is that a few small payload sizes are
> negligible to one single max payload.
>
> >
> >>
> >>> don't need to use payload buffers at all (eg flush, fsync, rmdir,
> >>
> >> Could be handled by multi buf sizes as well.
> >
> > These requests don't require payload buffers.
> >
> > This series already handles this case (eg not assigning a buffer from
> > the pool to the request).
>
> It handles exactly one additional case - zero payload size.
>
> >>
> >>> etc).  As well for registered buffers / buffer pinning, it is more
> >>> optimal for the buffer pool to be allocated contiguously and pinned
> >>> contiguously rather than passed in one by one as a 1:1 entry:buffer
> >>> mapping.
> >>
> >> I don't think how the buffer is passed to fuse-client/kernel matters for
> >> (userspace) allocation. I had written before that libfuse's buffer
> >> allocation is not ideal, I had it continuous in initial development,
> >> then run into bug and for simplicity changed to per entry allocation.
> >> Switching back to larger allocations is simple.
> >> Regarding page mapping/pinning, why does it matter if the buffer in
> >> kernel is known as per entry or per allocation (assuming payload buffers
> >> are page aligned)?
> >
> > It does matter how the buffers are passed to fuse for page pinning.
> > Please take a look at patch 8 ([PATCH v3 08/10] fuse: support
> > registered buffers as buffer pools in io-uring) [1]. The buffer pool
> > is contiguously allocated and registered as an io-uring registered
> > buffer, and then passed to fuse and by that point, the buffers have
> > already been pinned by io-uring.
> >
> > Even irrespective of page pinning, it would still be good to pass the
> > buffers contiguously up front as a pool instead of per-ent, as the
> > purpose of this series is to decouple the 1:1 entry:buffer mapping. Eg
> > if the request an ent is servicing does not require buffers, a buffer
> > should not be reserved for that ent, amongst other benefits of a
> > decoupled mapping.
> >
> > [1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260522205823.1597313-9-joannelkoong@gmail.com/
> >
> >>
> >> I think the more important part for buf rings are pinned headers. For 4K
> >> pages the loss is maybe still acceptable and the remaining size could be
> >> used for small requests and I guess would have a perf benefit. For 64K
> >> pages the loss is quite a lot.
> >
> > That is orthogonal to this series. This series focuses on payload
> > buffers, and header pinning is not part of this series as
> > bufpools/bufrings do not need pinned headers, as it makes no
> > difference for performance. As mentioned in the previous message, when
> > I was working on a separate series for removing the dispatch overhead,
> > I implemented header pinning as part of that, as that is when header
> > pinning becomes actually useful.
>
> Yes, header pinning is orthogonal, with the exception that ring entries
> of different payload sizes provide the same feature as buffer pools and
> with that buffer pools would not be a requirement anymore.

Buffer pools / buffer rings make entries generic (can service any
size), whereas per-entry different sizes locks each entry to a
specific size. I don't think ents and buffers should be statically
coupled 1:1.

>
> >> Maybe let's summarize
> >>
> >> Bufrings
> >> - Fixed size, allocated at startup, cannot be easily increased or decreased
> >> - Allows 0 size payloads (FUSE requests that only need the header)
> >>
> >> Entries with payload
> >> - Allows more flexible setup - fuse server _could_ (not implemented as
> >> of now) create entries based on incoming IO.
> >>
> >> Neither provides real multi-size buffers per ring.

>
> I just don't see "reduces the memory usage" - there are few op code that
> do not need payload buffers. Do you see that exactly these op code
> disturb your workflow and you do need additional entries/buffers for
> those? I do see a lot of lookup/revalidate/getxattr, but not the op
> codes without payload. Equally I see IO that does not max payload for a
> lot of requests types - anything namespace related and reads/writes
> below max payload size.
>
> In the end, what I'm struggling with is that the "zero payload" is just
> one special case of different IO sizes and the entire code and flow is
> changed for this one special case, without a discussion forehand how to
> handle the other cases. And that one special case comes up rarely in my
> own testing and use cases.

I think we're talking past each other. Buffer pools aren't about "zero
payload" as a special case - they're about decoupling buffers from
entries in general. Zero-payload requests are one beneficiary, but the
design enables incremental consumption and multi-size buffers as
future work precisely because of this decoupling.

Thanks,
Joanne

View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Bernd Schubert Reviewed-by Neutral LLM
Bernd Schubert questioned whether header pinning is still possible, noting that if it's not done, io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() cannot be avoided, resulting in a context switch for sync requests. He also proposed an alternative approach where the payload would be registered per ring entry and only the header would become a buffer ring.

Hi Joanne,

On 5/22/26 23:14, Joanne Koong wrote:
> On Fri, May 22, 2026 at 1:59\u202fPM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>>
>> Changelog
>> ---------
>> v2: https://lore.kernel.org/linux-fsdevel/20260402162840.2989717-1-joannelkoong@gmail.com/
>> v2 -> v3:
>> * Rework the uapis to be more ergonomic. Use io-uring registered buffers
>>   infrastructure instead of doing pinning logic in fuse. Get rid of header
>>   pinning as that makes no real perf difference
>> * Rename from "buffer ring" to buffer pool. Logic is the same, just different
>>   naming

short question, I didn't check the code is header pinning still
*possible*? The problem if we do not pin headers is that
io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() cannot be avoided, as the application
cannot write into fuse-server buffers and headers without pre-pinned
pages. And io_uring_cmd_complete_in_task() basically means a context
switch for all sync requests.

> 
> Just to reiterate, the logic is the same between this version and the
> previous versions. I just renamed it to "buffer pool" to decouple the
> implementation detail from the concept since it's uapi facing. The
> buffer pool is managed as a buffer ring internally.
> 
> Bernd, it would be much appreciated if you could take a look at this
> series when you have the time. I'm targeting the June merge window and
> the io-uring dependent changes have already been queued up in Jens's
> tree since the last merge window.

I will try and right is probably a good time, almost through with
libfuse sync-init (remaining are bugs), Darricks series is also almost
ready. Although I don't promise this weekend - reserved for Darrick and
other libfuse changes.

One piece I had been pondering a bit about, though did not dive into the
code yet.
What is if payload would be registered per ring entry (as without your
changes) and just the header would become a buffer ring (so that could
be pinned that way without loosing one page per entry)?  For zero-copy
we could register entries without a payload? Advantage here would be
that that header buffer would determine max number of entries, only
little memory would be needed for over-subscribed entries, but entries
themselves could be registered dynamically on demand.


Thanks,
Bernd

Thanks,
Bernd

> buffer, and then passed to fuse and by that point, the buffers have
> already been pinned by io-uring.
> 
> Even irrespective of page pinning, it would still be good to pass the
> buffers contiguously up front as a pool instead of per-ent, as the
> purpose of this series is to decouple the 1:1 entry:buffer mapping. Eg
> if the request an ent is servicing does not require buffers, a buffer
> should not be reserved for that ent, amongst other benefits of a
> decoupled mapping.
> 
> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260522205823.1597313-9-joannelkoong@gmail.com/
> 
>>
>> I think the more important part for buf rings are pinned headers. For 4K
>> pages the loss is maybe still acceptable and the remaining size could be
>> used for small requests and I guess would have a perf benefit. For 64K
>> pages the loss is quite a lot.
> 
> That is orthogonal to this series. This series focuses on payload
> buffers, and header pinning is not part of this series as
> bufpools/bufrings do not need pinned headers, as it makes no
> difference for performance. As mentioned in the previous message, when
> I was working on a separate series for removing the dispatch overhead,
> I implemented header pinning as part of that, as that is when header
> pinning becomes actually useful.

Yes, header pinning is orthogonal, with the exception that ring entries
of different payload sizes provide the same feature as buffer pools and
with that buffer pools would not be a requirement anymore.

> 
>>
>>
>> Maybe let's summarize
>>
>> Bufrings
>> - Fixed size, allocated at startup, cannot be easily increased or decreased
>> - Allows 0 size payloads (FUSE requests that only need the header)
>>
>> Entries with payload
>> - Allows more flexible setup - fuse server _could_ (not implemented as
>> of now) create entries based on incoming IO.
>>
>> Neither provides real multi-size buffers per ring.
>>
> 
> I think perhaps there is a misunderstanding of the patches in this series.
> 
> This series implements buffer pools, which decouple the 1:1 mapping
> between ents and buffers that currently exists. Right now, ents and
> buffers are tightly coupled where each entry has its own dedicated
> payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where each buffer
> must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size.
> 
> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool where the buffers
> are not statically mapped to an ent at registration time. This
> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use
> fuse-io-uring. It is the necessary stepping stone for future
> optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption (allowing
> multiple entries to use non-overlapping regions of the same buffer
> concurrently) and supporting multi-size buffers per ring.

I just don't see "reduces the memory usage" - there are few op code that
do not need payload buffers. Do you see that exactly these op code
disturb your workflow and you do need additional entries/buffers for
those? I do see a lot of lookup/revalidate/getxattr, but not the op
codes without payload. Equally I see IO that does not max payload for a
lot of requests types - anything namespace related and reads/writes
below max payload size.

In the end, what I'm struggling with is that the "zero payload" is just
one special case of different IO sizes and the entire code and flow is
changed for this one special case, without a discussion forehand how to
handle the other cases. And that one special case comes up rarely in my
own testing and use cases.


Thanks,
Bernd

Ongoing Submitted 2026-05-19 Last comment 2026-05-26 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v1 1/2] fuse: re-lock request before replacing page cache folio 2 patches
Show 1 individual patches
  • [2/2] fuse: re-lock request before returning from fuse_ref_folio() Submitted 2026-05-19
Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a use-after-free issue in the fuse kernel module by re-locking the request before replacing the page cache folio. The problem arises when the subsequent copy chain logic accesses the fuse_io_args after it has been freed. The fix involves calling lock_request() before replace_page_cache_folio() to ensure the request is locked on the success path.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Miklos Szeredi ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Acked-by Positive LLM
Identified the use-after-free issue and confirmed that the patch fixes it. Approved the patch for application.
Show original comment 
On Tue, 19 May 2026 at 07:41, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> fuse_try_move_folio() unlocks the request on entry but does not
> re-lock it on the success path. This means fuse_chan_abort() can end the
> request and free the fuse_io_args (eg fuse_readpages_end()) while the
> subsequent copy chain logic after fuse_try_move_folio() accesses the
> fuse_io_args, leading to use-after-free issues.
>
> Fix this by calling lock_request() before replace_page_cache_folio().
> This ensures the request is locked on the success path which will
> prevent the fuse_io_args from being freed while the later copying logic
> runs, and also ensures that the ap->folios[i]->mapping is never null
> since ap->folios[i] will always point to the newfolio after
> replace_page_cache_folio().

Last comment 2026-05-26 Re: [PATCH v3 00/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds support for io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy to the FUSE file system. The goal is to decouple the one-to-one mapping between entries and buffers, allowing for more efficient handling of requests that don't require payload buffers. The patches also address issues related to page pinning and buffer allocation, with a focus on passing buffers contiguously as a pool instead of per-entry. This series aims to simplify the kernel side and improve performance by reducing unnecessary complexity and overhead.

Signals: requested changes, design discussion, questioned efficiency
Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Bernd Schubert Neutral LLM
The author addresses Bernd Schubert's concern that statically coupling entries to buffers 1:1 as a fixed mapping introduces unnecessary complexity and inflexibility, by explaining that this series decouples the concepts of entries and buffers, allowing for dynamic addition of generic entries not tied to a particular buffer size. Author responded to concerns about the need for dynamic buffer allocation, explaining that buffer pools decouple entries from specific sizes, making them generic and able to service any size request. Author clarified that buffer pools in the patch series are designed to decouple buffers from entries, enabling incremental consumption and multi-size buffers as future work, not just a special case for zero-payload requests.
Show original comment 
It's not just about the memory savings. Fundamentally, I believe it's
the wrong abstraction to statically couple ents to buffers 1:1 as a
fixed mapping. Entries and buffers serve different purposes. Entries
handle request flow, buffers hold data. Statically coupling them 1:1
is inflexible, introduces unnecessary complexity, and imo has no added
benefit. This series decouples these two concepts. It allows
dynamically adding ents in the future as *generic* ents not tied to a
particular buffer size (eg the added ent can be used to service *any*
request size, not just the size of the buffer it's tied to). With
tying ents to buffers, the server is stuck with a static distribution
that can't adapt to workload changes.

---

Buffer pools / buffer rings make entries generic (can service any
size), whereas per-entry different sizes locks each entry to a
specific size. I don't think ents and buffers should be statically
coupled 1:1.

---

I think we're talking past each other. Buffer pools aren't about "zero
payload" as a special case - they're about decoupling buffers from
entries in general. Zero-payload requests are one beneficiary, but the
design enables incremental consumption and multi-size buffers as
future work precisely because of this decoupling.

Thanks,
Joanne

Re: [PATCH v3 00/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-05-29 Re: [PATCH v3 00/10] fuse: add io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds support for io-uring buffer pools and zero-copy in the FUSE (Filesystem in Userspace) kernel module. The current implementation reserves a full 1 MiB payload buffer for each entry, which can lead to significant memory waste on systems with many entries. Two approaches are proposed: decoupling entries from payload buffers by using a shared pool of dynamically selected and recycled buffers, or allowing entries to be registered with different payload buffer sizes at setup time. The goal is to reduce memory usage and improve performance for FUSE-based file systems.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants

Last comment 2026-05-27 Re: [PATCH] fuse: disable default bdi strictlimiting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch disables default strictlimiting in the fuse (Filesystem in Userspace) module to prevent performance regressions caused by excessive write throttling due to large folio sizes and limited dirty budgets. The current implementation can lead to severe performance issues, including oscillating writes between freerunning and full throttling, especially on systems with limited RAM or cgroups with memory constraints. By disabling strictlimiting, the patch aims to prevent misbehaving servers from accumulating too many dirty pages, which can degrade write throughput for other filesystems.

5 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

I'm hoping we're able to keep things as simple as possible by just
getting rid of strictlimiting by default for privileged servers on the
kernel side, without needing any module parameters or libfuse changes,
but I'm not sure if Miklos is comfortable with that. imo if the server
is privileged, it can be trusted to not misbehave and should be given
the same kind of capabilities as a local filesystem. But I guess there
might be the possibility of user regressions if it turns out people's
privileged fuse servers are accidentally buggy.

I think the module parameter makes sense if admins trust unprivileged
servers enough to turn off strictlimiting globally. Otherwise, I think
Miklos just mentioned there's already a /sys/class/bdi interface that
lets admins disable strictlimit on a per-mount basis, so I think it
would be cleaner (though more annoying on the userspace side :)) if
libfuse used this to turn it off by default. The max supported folio
order is set per-inode when the inode is first looked up, not at mount
time, so the admin can disable strictlimit via sysfs after mount
(which is when the bdi sysfs entry gets created) and new inodes will
pick up large folio support from there.

Thanks,
Joanne

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series (v4) aims to switch the THP shrinker from its current open-coded deferred split queue to using the list_lru data structure. The existing implementation has issues, including being non-NUMA-aware and complicated in its interactions with other code. Switching to list_lru fixes these problems, simplifies the code, and streamlines the interaction between the THP shrinker and LRU state. The series includes several cleanup patches (1-4) that prepare for this change, followed by extensions to the list_lru API (5-6) and finally the switch itself in patch 8.

Show original comment 
Can the shrinker bit end up on the wrong memcg here?

deferred_split_folio() takes the lock via list_lru_lock_irqsave() with
the original folio_memcg() of the folio. If that memcg is dying and
already reparented, lock_list_lru_of_memcg() walks up parent_mem_cgroup()

the locked sublist where the folio was queued.

Over here, you will end up reporting success on allocation failure.

On 28/05/2026 15:02, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> On Thu, May 28, 2026 at 02:32:06PM +0100, Usama Arif wrote:
>>
>>
>> On 27/05/2026 21:45, Johannes Weiner wrote:
>>> The deferred split queue handles cgroups in a suboptimal fashion. The
>>> queue is per-NUMA node or per-cgroup, not the intersection. That means
>>> on a cgrouped system, a node-restricted allocation entering reclaim
>>> can end up splitting large pages on other nodes:
>>>
>>>         alloc/unmap
>>>           deferred_split_folio()
>>>             list_add_tail(memcg->split_queue)
>>>             set_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>>>
>>>         for_each_zone_zonelist_nodemask(restricted_nodes)
>>>           mem_cgroup_iter()
>>>             shrink_slab(node, memcg)
>>>               shrink_slab_memcg(node, memcg)
>>>                 if test_shrinker_bit(memcg, node, deferred_shrinker_id)
>>>                   deferred_split_scan()
>>>                     walks memcg->split_queue
>>>
>>> The shrinker bit adds an imperfect guard rail. As soon as the cgroup
>>> has a single large page on the node of interest, all large pages owned
>>> by that memcg, including those on other nodes, will be split.
>>>
>>> list_lru properly sets up per-node, per-cgroup lists. As a bonus, it
>>> streamlines a lot of the list operations and reclaim walks. It's used
>>> widely by other major shrinkers already. Convert the deferred split
>>> queue as well.
>>>
>>> The list_lru per-memcg heads are instantiated on demand when the first
>>> object of interest is allocated for a cgroup, by calling
>>> folio_memcg_alloc_deferred(). Add calls to where splittable pages are
>>> created: anon faults, swapin faults, khugepaged collapse.
>>>
>>> These calls create all possible node heads for the cgroup at once, so
>>> the migration code (between nodes) doesn't need any special care.
>>>
>>> Reported-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>>> Tested-by: Mikhail Zaslonko <zaslonko@linux.ibm.com>
>>> Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>
>>> Reviewed-by: Lorenzo Stoakes (Oracle) <ljs@kernel.org>

  • [?/8] mm/memcontrol, page_counter: move stock from mem_cgroup to page_counter Neutral Sashiko
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch optimizes the memsw stock for cgroup v1 by introducing an independent stock for the memsw page counter, which is charged separately from the memory page counter. This change improves caching and reduces the worst-case scenario where both counters exceed their limits, requiring a hierarchical charge. The user-visible effect is that reported memsw usage may temporarily be lower than memory usage due to batching of stock charges.

Last comment 2026-05-29 Re: [PATCH] mm: don't allow empty relative nodemask in mpol_relative_nodemask() Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch prevents empty relative nodemasks in mpol_relative_nodemask() by adding a check for the node weight being zero, which is not sufficient as it allows empty nodemasks for certain policy modes. Instead, the full policy-nodemask checking section from mpol_new() should be called from mpol_set_nodemask(). This ensures that all places where mpol_relative_nodemask() is called perform the same validation as mpol_new().

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Yury Norov ↳ replying to Joshua Hahn Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Yury Norov noted that nodes_fold() cannot handle an empty bitmap (sz == 0), and proposed two options to address this issue: disallowing empty relmaps in mpol_relative_nodemask() or following the behavior of nodes_onto() by clearing the ret mask and bailing out when an empty relmask is encountered. reviewer questioned the approach of checking for empty relative nodemask in mpol_relative_nodemask(), pointing out that MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES is set only when called, and that rebind case is not handled correctly
Show original comment 
Hi Joshua.

Indeed, quick and dirty shot.

The problem is that nodes_fold() can't work with the sz == 0. In
other words, folding to a 0-bit bitmap is an error. We don't check
that on bitmaps level because it's an internal helper, and it's a
caller's responsibility to validate the parameters.

nodes_onto(), or more specifically bitmap_onto(), is a different
story. In case of empty relmap, the function actually clears all the
bits in dst and returns.

I see 2 options to move this forward.

1. Simply disallow empty relmap in mpol_relative_nodemask(). There's
no valid cases for it, AFAIK, so the nodes_fold() limitation looks
reasonable. We can consider it as a new policy.

We've got 2 users for mpol_relative_nodemask(). In mpol_set_nodemask()
we can simply propagate the error; and in mpol_rebind_nodemask() we
can throw a warning and do nothing.

2. Follow the spirit of the nodes_onto(), and in case of empty
relmask, clean the ret mask and bail out

I'm in a favor for the 1st option, because empty relmask looks buggy
anyways.

---

Not sure I understand this. The mpol_relative_nodemask() is called
only if MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES is set. In mpol_rebind_nodemask(), if
both MPOL_F_STATIC_NODES and MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES are set, the former
wins. How would the RELATIVE mode mess with the others?

The mpol_new() code seemingly tries to disable empty nodes in case of
MPOL_DEFAILT and MPOL_PREFERRED + MPOL_F_RELATIVE_NODES, but obviously
it doesn't work very well in the rebind case.

Anyways, I'm not really deep in mempolicy domain, so please educate me if
I miss something.

Thanks,
Yury

Patches Submitted (2)
Ongoing Submitted 2026-05-19 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH] mm/compaction: cap compact_gap() at COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX Neutral LLM

This patch caps the `compact_gap()` function at `COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX` to prevent over-reserving memory and improve compaction success rates. The current implementation returns a gap that scales exponentially by order, but this can lead to unnecessary reclaim attempts and failed compaction checks. By capping the gap, the patch aims to align it with the actual capacity of the compaction scanner.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Vlastimil Babka (SUSE) ↳ replying to JP Kobryn (Meta) Needs Work LLM
Raised concerns that capping the `compact_gap()` function may mask genuine memory pressure and questioned the patch's impact on compaction success rates.
Signals: memory pressure, compaction success
Ongoing Submitted 2026-05-19 Last comment 2026-05-25 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH] mm/page_alloc: skip high atomic reservation at or below costly order Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's page allocation mechanism to prevent high atomic reservations for allocations at or below a certain order (PAGE_ALLOC_COSTLY_ORDER). This is done by adding a check in the reserve_highatomic_pageblock function, which skips reserving entire pageblocks for smaller atomic allocations. The goal of this change is to reduce fragmentation and improve the success rate of large memory allocations, particularly for Transparent Huge Pages (THP) on systems with plenty of free memory.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Andrew Morton ↳ replying to JP (Meta) Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Andrew Morton questioned whether skipping HighAtomic reservation for orders 1-3 would break anti-fragmentation guarantees, potentially leading to dropped packets or I/O errors in subsystems like the network stack or BTRFS.
Show original comment 
Sashiko asked

: Does skipping the HighAtomic reservation for orders 1-3 break the
: anti-fragmentation guarantees for these atomic allocations?
: 
: The MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC reserve protects high-order atomic allocations
: from failing under fragmentation by taking ownership of the entire
: pageblock.
: 
: If order-1 through order-3 atomic allocations fall back to stealing
: pages, but the pageblock remains in its original migratetype, won't
: order-0 non-atomic allocations consume the remaining contiguous space?
: 
: Under memory pressure, this could leave no contiguous blocks for atomic
: allocations to steal.  Because these atomic allocations cannot trigger
: direct reclaim or compaction, they might fail, potentially leading to
: dropped packets or I/O errors in subsystems like the network stack or
: BTRFS.
: 
: Could background compaction or khugepaged be used to unreserve
: HighAtomic blocks dynamically instead of disabling the reserve for
: these orders?
View on lore ↗
Signals: requested changes, potential issues
Johannes Weiner ↳ replying to JP (Meta) Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Johannes Weiner questioned the patch's effectiveness, noting that disabling highatomic reserve for costly orders didn't seem to matter and suggesting that maxing out reserves is unnecessary as highatomic allocations are typically short-lived.
Show original comment 
This is an interesting patch. A couple of thoughts:

1. You disabled the highatomic reserve for this workload and it didn't
seem to matter. Presumably <costly orders don't need the protection.

2. Maxing out the reserves is odd. ALLOC_HIGHATOMIC allocations will
try reserved space first, and I'd expect things that are commonly
highatomic to be short-lived. Why don't we stop with a couple of
claimed highatomic blocks that get continuously recycled?

3. The impact on THP and compaction success rate is pretty
extreme. How can 1% of memory throw such a wrench into the gears?

Have you tried this with other workloads?
View on lore ↗
Signals: requested changes, suggested improvements
JP (Meta) (author) ↳ replying to Andrew Morton Needs Work LLM
Author acknowledged reviewer's feedback that smaller atomic allocations do not benefit from the high atomic reservation, agreeing that it should be skipped for orders 1-3. Author acknowledges that smaller atomic allocations do not reserve entire pageblocks, as observed in experiments where there were no failures for orders 1-3 on a fragmented system. Author addressed Andrew Morton's concern that the patch would prevent smaller atomic allocations from being movable, explaining that the patch actually keeps these pageblocks movable and improves compaction Author addressed a concern that reserved HighAtomic pageblocks are not treated as precious resources, explaining that under real memory pressure they are already given up by the kernel's unreserve mechanism. Author responded to a concern about introducing additional overhead by explaining that the current patch already reduces reclaim and arguing against adding more scanning/overhead/stats.
Show original comment 
The data included in the changelog supports the claim that the reserve
does not provide a benefit at these orders. Even on fragmented systems,
orders 1-3 have plenty of pages available.

---

In the experiments, there were no failures for orders 1-3 despite a
fragmented system and no reserved pageblocks for these orders.

---

With the patch, these pageblocks stay movable. So if fallback is needed,
moveable pages can still be taken. But the patch actually improves
compaction so contiguous space is increased overall.

---

Reserved HighAtomic pageblocks are not currently treated as a precious
resource. Under real memory pressure, the kernel already gives them up -
the unreserve mechanism kicks in and converts the HighAtomic pageblocks
back to their original migrate type.

---

This would call for extra scanning/overhead/stats. The patch reduces
reclaim, for example. A new scanner feels like going in the opposite
direction.

Hi Kiryl,

On 2026-05-25T13:28:11+0100, Kiryl Shutsemau wrote:

---

Please have a look at the CONTRIBUTING file, and the CONTRIBUTING.d
directory.  In particular, it would be nice to see more useful hunk
contexts, which can be done following this documentation:

<https://git.kernel.org/pub/scm/docs/man-pages/man-pages.git/tree/CONTRIBUTING.d/git#n11>

---

Please use \[em] instead of \(em, and use spaces around them as if they
were parentheses --like this--.

Also, please use semantic newlines.  See man-pages(7):

$ MANWIDTH=64 man man-pages | awk '/Use semantic newlines/,/^$/'
   Use semantic newlines
     In  the  source  of a manual page, new sentences should be
     started on new lines, long sentences should be split  into
     lines at clause breaks (commas, semicolons, colons, and so
     on),  and  long  clauses  should be split at phrase bound‐
     aries.  This convention, sometimes known as "semantic new‐
     lines", makes it easier to  see  the  effect  of  patches,
     which  often operate at the level of individual sentences,
     clauses, or phrases.

That would be:

	When registered with
	.B UFFDIO_REGISTER_MODE_RWP
	mode,
	user-space will receive a page-fault notification
	on any access
	\[em]read or write\[em]
	to a present page within the range.

BTW, about the last line, I think you may want to mean
s/present page/page present/, with inverted order of the words, that is,
the page is present within the range, right?

---

'By default' should be followed by a comma.  Also, because of semantic
newlines, I'd move 'until' to the next line.  Thus:

	By default,
	the faulted thread will be stopped from execution
	until user space removes the protection using a

---

We say user space, as two words.  'user-space' is used when it works as
a compound adjective (usual English rules).

---

Again semantic newlines:

	...
	was negotiated,
	the kernel restores access in place
	and the faulted thread continues without blocking.

A rule of thumb when separating lines semantically is that it should be
relatively easy to understand full lines without reading the surrounding
lines.

---

s/returns/fails with/

I user space, we transform the return value to an errno code, and return
-1.

---

This should probably be read-write-protect mode, for consistency with
write-prtect mode.  Or maybe read/write-protect mode.

---

We changed to using .P some years ago.  It works exactly the same, and
is easier to type.

---

This is weird again.  Please don't have more than one identifier per
line.

Show original comment 
From: "Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta)" <kas@kernel.org>

change_non_present_huge_pmd() rewrites a writable device-private PMD
swap entry into a readable one without carrying pmd_swp_uffd_wp()
across. The PTE-level change_softleaf_pte() does this correctly;
mirror that here, matching what copy_huge_pmd() does for the fork
path. Without the carry, a plain mprotect() over a UFFD_WP-marked
device-private THP strips the bit and the trap is bypassed on
swap-in.

Fixes: 368076f52ebe ("mm/huge_memory: add device-private THP support to PMD operations")
Cc: stable@vger.kernel.org
Reported-by: Sashiko AI review <sashiko-bot@kernel.org>
Signed-off-by: Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org>
---
 mm/huge_memory.c | 2 ++
 1 file changed, 2 insertions(+)

diff --git a/mm/huge_memory.c b/mm/huge_memory.c
index 42b86e8ab7c0..b7c895b1d366 100644
--- a/mm/huge_memory.c
+++ b/mm/huge_memory.c
@@ -2663,6 +2663,8 @@ static void change_non_present_huge_pmd(struct mm_struct *mm,
 	} else if (softleaf_is_device_private_write(entry)) {
 		entry = make_readable_device_private_entry(swp_offset(entry));
 		newpmd = swp_entry_to_pmd(entry);
+		if (pmd_swp_uffd_wp(*pmd))
+			newpmd = pmd_swp_mkuffd_wp(newpmd);
 	} else {
 		newpmd = *pmd;
 	}

Neutral LLM

This patch series adds userfaultfd support for tracking the working set of VM guest memory, enabling a VMM to identify cold pages and evict them to tiered or remote storage. The approach introduces a new userfaultfd registration mode (MODE_RWP) that uses PAGE_NONE on PTEs to mark pages as inaccessible while keeping them resident. This allows the kernel to deliver UFFD_PAGEFAULT_FLAG_RWP messages to registered handlers, which can resolve faults with UFFDIO_RWPROTECT.

Dave Hansen Neutral LLM

Show original comment 
On 5/27/26 05:05, Kiryl Shutsemau (Meta) wrote:
...
> -	/* Update part of the register affected by the emulated instruction */
> -	regs->ax &= ~mask;
> +	/*
> +	 * IN writes the result into a sub-register of RAX. Only the
> +	 * 32-bit form zero-extends; the smaller forms leave the upper
> +	 * bits untouched:
> +	 *
> +	 *   insn  dest  size  bits written     bits preserved
> +	 *   inb   AL    1     RAX[ 7: 0]       RAX[63: 8]
> +	 *   inw   AX    2     RAX[15: 0]       RAX[63:16]
> +	 *   inl   EAX   4     RAX[63: 0]       (none, zero-extended)
> +	 *
> +	 * 'mask' only covers the low 'size' bytes, which is exactly the
> +	 * range affected for size 1 and 2. For size 4 the write also
> +	 * clears RAX[63:32], so widen the clear-mask.
> +	 */
> +	if (size == 4)
> +		regs->ax = 0;
> +	else
> +		regs->ax &= ~mask;
> +

Is there any way we could do this with fewer comments and more code?

I mean, there's only three cases. Why have;

	u64 mask = GENMASK(BITS_PER_BYTE * size - 1, 0);

When there are only 3 possible cases:

	1 => 0xf
	2 => 0xff
	4 => 0xffff

and one of those cases needs a special case on top of it.

Maybe something like this?

	/* Clear out part of RAX so part of args.r11 can be OR'd in: */
	switch (size) {
	case 1:
		/* inb consumes lower 8 bits of r11: */
		regs->ax &= ~GENMASK_ULL(7, 0);
		args.r11 &=  GENMASK_ULL(7, 0);
		break;
	case 2:
		/* inw consumes lower 16 bits of r11: */
		regs->ax &= ~GENMASK_ULL(15, 0);
		args.r11 &=  GENMASK_ULL(15, 0);
		break;
	case 4:
		/* inl is weird and zeros the whole register: */
		regs->ax &= ~GENMASK_ULL(63, 0);
		/* But only consumes 32-bits from r11: */
		args.r11 &=  GENMASK_ULL(31, 0);
		break;
	default:
		/* Probable TDX module bug. Illegal in[bwl] size: */
		WARN_ON_ONCE(1);
		success = 0;
	}

	if (success)
		regs->ax |= args.r11;

It might need a temporary variable for args.r11, but you get the point.
That's basically the data from the comment but written as code.

Last comment 2026-05-28 Re: [PATCH v3 2/2] x86/tdx: Fix zero-extension for 32-bit port I/O Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a bug in the TDX (Trusted Domain Extensions) code on x86 architecture, specifically for 32-bit port I/O operations. The issue was that the code did not properly handle zero-extension when reading from ports of different sizes. The patch introduces a switch statement to handle this correctly, ensuring that the result is written to the correct sub-register of RAX based on the size of the operation.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Dave Hansen ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Dave Hansen expressed concern that the code is overly clever and may be unintelligible, suggesting a simpler approach using inline assembly to match the IN[BWL] behavior.
Show original comment 
Is this intentionally clever to only work on little endian, or
accidentally clever? This seems like a great IOCCC thing to do, but it's
far too clever for my taste.

I mean, it's making a pointer to a 64-bit value with an 8-bit name and
casting that to an 8-bit pointer and then assigning that to a 32-bit
value cast to a u8.

Is it just my tiny brain that thinks this will be unintelligible on Monday?

How about we just make the CPU do the thinking for us? IN[BWL] and
MOV[BWL] have the same semantics here, right? So even if 'rax' and 'val'
are 64-bit values here, the following should have all the right
behaviors, I think.

I generally loathe inline assembly. But we have a CPU that kinda knows
the rules already. No need for us to laboriously reimplement it. Right?

Thanks to the friendly LLM that knows inline assembly better than I do.
The resulting compiled assembly looks right to me.

/*
 * Use MOV[BWL] to/from registers to match the IN[BWL] behavior
 * including the fact that INL zeros the upper 64-bits while
 * IN[BW] don't zero anything.
 */

    switch (size) {
    case 1:
	// Just write 1 byte of RAX:
        __asm__ volatile ("movb %b1, %b0" : "+q"(rax)
                                          : "q"(val));
        break;
    case 2:
	// Write 2 bytes of RAX:
        __asm__ volatile ("movw %w1, %w0" : "+r"(rax)
                                          : "r"(val));
        break;
    case 4:
	// Write 'val' into lower 32 bits. Zero the upper 32 bits:
        __asm__ volatile ("movl %k1, %k0" : "=r"(rax)
                                          : "r"(val));
        break;
    default:
	// WARN
    }

Thoughts?

David Laight ↳ replying to Kiryl Shutsemau Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer David Laight expressed dissatisfaction with the verbosity of the patch, specifically mentioning that the use of GENMASK_ULL() macros makes it hard to follow. Reviewer suggested replacing the switch statement in handle_in() with arithmetic code to make it more readable and concise.
Show original comment 
On Thu, 28 May 2026 11:14:38 +0100
Kiryl Shutsemau <kas@kernel.org> wrote:

---

Just write it as normal arithmetic code:

	/* IN writes the result into a sub-register of RAX. */
	switch (size) {
	case 1:
		regs->ax = (regs->ax & ~0xfful) | (val & 0xff);
		break;
	case 2:
		regs->ax = (regs->ax & ~0xfffful) | (val & 0xffff);
		break;
	case 4:
	default:
		/* 32bit 'INB' will zero the high bits. */
		regs->ax = val
		break;
	}

Succinct, obvious and readable.

-- David

Leo Martins

3 items
Patches Submitted (3)
[PATCH v2 0/2] btrfs: skip COW for written extent buffers Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series optimizes Btrfs by skipping redundant copy-on-write (COW) operations on extent buffers that have already been written back to disk due to memory pressure. The optimization relies on the BTRFS_HEADER_FLAG_WRITTEN flag, which is set when a buffer is written back to disk. This prevents unnecessary re-COW of these buffers during subsequent btrfs_search_slot() calls, reducing block reservations and improving throughput under memory pressure. Additionally, the patch fixes a latent issue in qgroup snapshot accounting by separating root item persistence from finalization steps.

[PATCH v2 1/2] btrfs: don't finalize fs roots during qgroup snapshot accounting Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the Btrfs file system to prevent finalizing file system roots during qgroup snapshot accounting. The original code would prematurely clear flags and free metadata, leading to incorrect state and potential corruption of snapshots. To fix this, a new function `qgroup_commit_fs_roots()` is introduced, which iterates over dirty roots and updates their root items without triggering finalization side effects. This approach ensures that the transaction remains in progress and allows for future optimizations to rely on the FORCE_COW flag correctly.

[PATCH v2 2/2] btrfs: skip COW for written extent buffers allocated in current transaction Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch optimizes Btrfs by skipping unnecessary copy-on-write (COW) operations when a buffer is written in the current transaction. When memory pressure causes writeback of a recently COW'd buffer, Btrfs sets a flag indicating it has been written. Subsequent searches then re-COW the buffer unnecessarily, leading to COW amplification and potential performance degradation. The patch addresses this issue by re-dirtying the buffer and re-registering its range in the transaction's dirty_pages when the generation matches the current transaction. This approach ensures that the buffer is not unnecessarily re-written, while maintaining crash safety and proper handling of dirty metadata.

Patches Submitted (1)
[RFC PATCH 0/5] mm, swap: Virtual Swap Space (Swap Table Edition) 5 patches
Show 5 individual patches
  • [1/5] mm, swap: add virtual swap device infrastructure
  • [2/5] mm, swap: support zswap and zeroswap as vswap backends
  • [3/5] mm, swap: support physical swap as a vswap backend
  • [4/5] mm, swap: only charge physical swap entries
  • [5/5] mm, swap: add debugfs counters for vswap
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series introduces a virtual swap space implementation using Kairui Song's swap table infrastructure. The goal is to decouple PTE swap entries from physical swap backing, allowing for compression by zswap without pre-allocating physical swap slots. This addresses three key requirements: no backend coupling, dynamic swap space allocation, and efficient backend transfer. The patch series reimplements the virtual swap space concept on top of the swap table infrastructure and is feature-complete relative to a previous baseline. It allows for pages to be compressed by zswap without pre-allocating physical swap slots, simplifying operational provisioning and maximizing in-memory compression backend utilization.

Yosry Ahmed ↳ replying to Nhat Pham Contentious LLM
Strongly disagreed with the use of pre-compression size, suggesting it's counter-intuitive and proposing a separate interface if used. Also suggested that actual compressed size should be used for proactive writeback.
Show original comment 
On Fri, May 29, 2026 at 12:58:09PM -0700, Nhat Pham wrote:
> On Tue, May 26, 2026 at 4:46\u202fAM Hao Jia <jiahao.kernel@gmail.com> wrote:
> >
> > From: Hao Jia <jiahao1@lixiang.com>
> >
> > Zswap currently writes back pages to backing swap reactively, triggered
> > either by the shrinker or when the pool reaches its size limit. There is
> > no mechanism to control the amount of writeback for a specific memory
> > cgroup. However, users may want to proactively write back zswap pages,
> > e.g., to free up memory for other applications or to prepare for
> > memory-intensive workloads.
> >
> > Introduce a "zswap_writeback_only" key to the memory.reclaim cgroup
> > interface. When specified, this key bypasses standard memory reclaim
> > and exclusively performs proactive zswap writeback up to the requested
> > budget. If omitted, the default reclaim behavior remains unchanged.
> >
> > Example usage:
> >   # Write back 100MB of pages from zswap to the backing swap
> >   echo "100M zswap_writeback_only" > memory.reclaim
> 
> Hmmm, so this 100MB is the pre-compression size? i.e if this 100 MB
> compresses to 25 MB, then you're only freeing 25 MB?
> 
> I'm ok-ish with this, but can you document it?

That's a good point. I think pre-compressed size doesn't make sense to
be honest. We should care about how much memory we are actually trying
to save by doing writeback here.

The pre-compressed size is only useful in determining the blast radius,
how many actual pages are going to have slower page faults now. But
then, I don't think there's a reasonable way for userspace to decide
that.

I understand passing in the compressed size is tricky because we need to
keep track of the size of the compressed pages we end up writing back,
but it should be doable.

If we really want pre-compressed size here, then yes we need to make it
very clear, and I vote that we use a separate interface in this case
because memory.reclaim having different meanings for the amount of
memory written to it is extremely counter-intuitive.

> 
> The rest seems solid to me, FWIW. I'll defer to Johannes and Yosry for
> opinions on zswap-only proactive reclaim.

Rik van Riel

11 items
Patches Submitted (7)
[PATCH v2] btrfs: allocate eb-attached btree pages as movable Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the way btrfs allocates pages for extent buffers to make them movable, which allows the page allocator to move these pages even when they are attached to a btree inode. The change involves passing the full GFP mask directly to functions that allocate pages, rather than having them calculate it from boolean flags. This is done to address fragmentation issues caused by btree_inode pages being pinned in unmovable pageblocks, which prevents hugepage allocation and leads to disk space waste. The patch targets a specific problem where ~28 GB of btree_inode pages are parked across many tainted superpageblocks on a test system, preventing 1 GiB hugepage allocation from those regions.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-05-18 Last comment 2026-05-28 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v2 0/4] iova: switch to augmented rbtree to avoid linear search 4 patches
Show 4 individual patches

  • [2/4] iova: drop dead cached_node / cached32_node infrastructure Submitted 2026-05-18

  • [4/4] iova: add KUnit test suite Submitted 2026-05-18
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series addresses performance issues caused by linear searches in alloc_iova() by converting the iova rbtree to an augmented rbtree, which maintains O(log n) complexity for all relevant operations. The change allows alloc_iova() to also be O(log n), preventing soft lockups that can occur when multiple CPUs fall into the linear search trap. The patches were written by Claude and reviewed by Rik van Riel, with additional testing and self-tests included.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Robin Murphy Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
reviewer expressed interest in the patch series and noted that some higher-level details stand out, but did not provide specific technical concerns or suggestions reviewer noted that the anchor node is necessary to ensure cached_node can be a valid starting point for allocation walks, and questioned its relevance if the algorithm is being completely changed Reviewer Robin Murphy expressed concerns that the allocation algorithm may not be optimal and suggested revising it to directly walk from the root towards the highest free space under the given limit_pfn for any value of limit_pfn, potentially eliminating the need for special cases like max32_alloc_size bodge. Additionally, he is cautious about growing struct iova unnecessarily due to concerns about iova_cache usage on busy systems. Reviewer Robin Murphy noted that the patch does not address DMA masks of 33-56 bits and suggested a general algorithm for handling such cases, which involves navigating the augmented rbtree in a specific way to find sufficient space. He also questioned whether an rbtree is still the best choice for this task.
Show original comment 
Hi Rik,

I'm certainly interested to see this series, as improving the allocator 
has been on my to-do list for a very long time now. It might take me a 
while to page all the details back in, but some higher-level things 
stand out already...

On 2026-05-18 7:05 pm, Rik van Riel wrote:

---

The main reason the anchor node exists is to ensure that cached_node can 
always be a valid place to start an allocation walk - if we're no longer 
using the latter, there's little point in keeping the former either, 
especially if it starts causing undesirable issues. It does also help as 
an optimisation to avoid a couple of special cases in the current 
algorithm, but again if we're completely changing the algorithm then 
those may well no longer apply either.

---

I would think that if we still need a special case for this then we 
don't really have the right allocation algorithm - I recall getting as 
far as concluding that it would be hard to do with the generic interval 
tree, but with the right subtree data I would think we ought to be able 
to walk directly from the root towards the highest free space under the 
given limit_pfn, for any value of limit_pfn. In fact I'd rather hope 
that the general case could get good enough to no longer need the 
max32_alloc_size bodge either (especially since pci_32bit_workaround 
will now already give up trying at all after the first failure).

Furthermore I'm also wary of growing struct iova any more than 
absolutely necessary, since people have sometimes complained about 
iova_cache usage being a large number on big, busy systems.

---

And what about all the 33 to 56-bit DMA masks? They're not all that 
uncommon and deserve some love too. FWIW I'd imagine a general algorithm 
should be something like:

- while pfn_lo > limit_pfn && left->max_gap >= size, go left
- while pfn_hi + size <= limit_pfn && right->max_gap >= size, go right
- if sufficient space to right, insert right and stop.
- if left->max_gap > size, go left, else go up and left
- repeat

(If anything that then leans towards maintaining a dummy node down at 
start_pfn, such that we could avoid a special case for allocating off 
the leftmost end of the tree...)

I guess another question to ask these days is perhaps whether rbtree is 
still even the best choice at all, or if something newer like maple tree 
might be able to be even better?

Thanks,
Robin.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series aims to improve the allocation of large memory blocks by dividing memory into superpageblocks and steering unmovable, reclaimable, and high-atomic allocations into those already tainted by such allocations. The goal is to leave as many 1GB superpageblocks available for movable allocations, which can be easily defragmented for regular or huge pages. Various strategies are employed to achieve this, including preferential allocation from tainted blocks, fallback to small pages for certain kernel allocations, and migration of movable memory when a block falls below a free threshold. This approach enables more extensive use of 2MB THPs and mTHPs, as well as reliable allocation of 1GB pages. The series is built on top of Johannes's PCPBuddy series, which aims to reduce the need for CPUs to get pages from zone free lists by relying on page block ownership.

Rik Riel (author) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed a concern that the zone ID passed to init_unavailable_range() may not match the actual zone spanning each PFN in memory holes between memblock regions, which can cause incorrect page flags and crashes. The author fixed this by looking up the correct zone for each PFN in the hole, which has no performance impact since it's init-only code running once at boot.
Show original comment 
init_unavailable_range() initializes struct pages for memory holes between
memblock regions.  It receives a zone ID from its caller, but that zone ID
is simply the last zone processed in memmap_init()'s iteration -- it does
not necessarily match the zone that actually spans each PFN in the hole.

When an unavailable range straddles a zone boundary (e.g. a hole between
DMA32 and Normal), all pages in the hole get tagged with the wrong zone in
page->flags.  Any later page_zone() call on such a page returns the wrong
zone, which can cause accounting confusion or crashes when code assumes the
returned zone is valid for that page.

Fix by looking up the correct zone for each PFN in the hole.  This is init-
only code running once at boot, so the per-page zone lookup has no
performance impact.

Signed-off-by: Rik van Riel <riel@surriel.com>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4.7 syzkaller

Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed
Rik Riel (author) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed a concern about hotplugged memory not being covered by superpageblocks, acknowledged the issue, and explained how they plan to fix it in a new function called resize_zone_superpageblocks(). This function will be called from move_pfn_range_to_zone() after the zone span has been updated. It will allocate a new superpageblock array with kvmalloc_node(), copy existing superpageblocks, and initialize new superpageblocks for the added range.
Show original comment 
setup_superpageblocks() is __init-only and uses memblock_alloc_node(), so
hotplugged memory that extends a zone's span has no superpageblock
coverage.  Pages in those regions would bypass superpageblock steering
entirely.

Add resize_zone_superpageblocks() which is called from
move_pfn_range_to_zone() after the zone span has been updated. It allocates
a new superpageblock array with kvmalloc_node() covering the full zone
span, copies existing superpageblocks (fixing up list head pointers), and
initializes new superpageblocks for the added range.

Use round-up division for partial pageblock counting to match
init_one_superpageblock().

ZONE_DEVICE is excluded since device pages should not participate in anti-
fragmentation steering.

Signed-off-by: Rik van Riel <riel@surriel.com>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4.7 syzkaller

+#ifdef CONFIG_MEMORY_HOTPLUG
+/**
+ * resize_zone_superpageblocks - grow superpageblock array for memory hotplug
+ * @zone: zone whose span has been extended by hotplug
+ *
+ * Called from move_pfn_range_to_zone() after resize_zone_range() has
+ * updated the zone's span.  Allocates a new superpageblock array covering
+ * the full zone span, copies existing superpageblocks (fixing up list heads),
+ * and initializes new superpageblocks for the added range.
+ *
+ * Must be called under mem_hotplug_lock (write).  No concurrent
+ * allocations can occur since the hotplugged pages are not yet online.
+ */
+void __meminit resize_zone_superpageblocks(struct zone *zone)
+{
+	unsigned long zone_start = zone->zone_start_pfn;
+	unsigned long zone_end = zone_start + zone->spanned_pages;
+	unsigned long new_sb_base, new_nr_sbs;
+	unsigned long old_offset;
+	struct superpageblock *old_sbs;
+	struct superpageblock *new_sbs;
+	bool old_kvmalloced;
+	size_t alloc_size;
+	unsigned long i;
+	int nid = zone_to_nid(zone);
+
+	if (!zone->spanned_pages)
+		return;
+
+	new_sb_base = ALIGN_DOWN(zone_start, SUPERPAGEBLOCK_NR_PAGES);
+	new_nr_sbs = (ALIGN(zone_end, SUPERPAGEBLOCK_NR_PAGES) - new_sb_base) >>
+		     SUPERPAGEBLOCK_ORDER;
+
+	/* Already covered? */
+	if (zone->superpageblocks &&
+	    new_sb_base == zone->superpageblock_base_pfn &&
+	    new_nr_sbs == zone->nr_superpageblocks)
+		return;
+
+	alloc_size = new_nr_sbs * sizeof(struct superpageblock);
+	new_sbs = kvmalloc_node(alloc_size, GFP_KERNEL | __GFP_ZERO, nid);
+	if (!new_sbs) {
+		pr_warn("Failed to allocate %zu bytes for zone %s superpageblocks\n",
+			alloc_size, zone->name);
+		return;
+	}
+
+	/*
+	 * Copy existing superpageblocks to their new position.
+	 * The old array covers [old_base, old_base + old_nr * SB_SIZE).
+	 * The new array covers [new_base, new_base + new_nr * SB_SIZE).
+	 * old_base >= new_base always (zone can only grow).
+	 */
+	if (zone->superpageblocks) {
+		old_offset = (zone->superpageblock_base_pfn - new_sb_base) >>
+			     SUPERPAGEBLOCK_ORDER;
+		memcpy(&new_sbs[old_offset], zone->superpageblocks,
+		       zone->nr_superpageblocks * sizeof(struct superpageblock));
+
+		/*
+		 * Fix up list_head pointers that were self-referencing
+		 * (empty lists) or pointing into the old array.
+		 */
+		for (i = old_offset; i < old_offset + zone->nr_superpageblocks; i++) {
+			struct superpageblock *sb = &new_sbs[i];
+
+			if (list_empty(&sb->list))
+				INIT_LIST_HEAD(&sb->list);
+			else
+				list_replace(&zone->superpageblocks[i - old_offset].list,
+					     &sb->list);
+		}
+	}
+
+	/* Initialize new superpageblocks (slots not covered by old array) */
+	for (i = 0; i < new_nr_sbs; i++) {
+		struct superpageblock *sb = &new_sbs[i];
+		bool is_old = false;
+
+		if (zone->superpageblocks) {
+			old_offset = (zone->superpageblock_base_pfn - new_sb_base) >>
+				     SUPERPAGEBLOCK_ORDER;
+			if (i >= old_offset &&
+			    i < old_offset + zone->nr_superpageblocks)
+				is_old = true;
+		}
+
+		if (is_old)
+			continue;
+
+		init_one_superpageblock(sb, zone,
+					new_sb_base + (i << SUPERPAGEBLOCK_ORDER),
+					zone_start, zone_end);
+	}
+
+	/*
+	 * Update existing superpageblocks whose nr_reserved may have
+	 * increased due to the zone span growing into them.
+	 */
+	if (zone->superpageblocks) {
+		old_offset = (zone->superpageblock_base_pfn - new_sb_base) >>
+			     SUPERPAGEBLOCK_ORDER;
+		for (i = old_offset; i < old_offset + zone->nr_superpageblocks; i++) {
+			struct superpageblock *sb = &new_sbs[i];
+			unsigned long sb_start = sb->start_pfn;
+			unsigned long sb_end = sb_start + SUPERPAGEBLOCK_NR_PAGES;
+			unsigned long pb_start = max(sb_start, zone_start);
+			unsigned long pb_end = min(sb_end, zone_end);
+			u16 new_pbs = (pb_end > pb_start) ?
+				((pb_end - pb_start + pageblock_nr_pages - 1) >>
+				 pageblock_order) : 0;
+			u16 old_pbs = sb->nr_free + sb->nr_unmovable +
+				sb->nr_reclaimable + sb->nr_movable +
+				sb->nr_reserved;
+
+			if (new_pbs > old_pbs)
+				sb->nr_reserved += new_pbs - old_pbs;
+		}
+	}
+
+	/* Swap in the new array */
+	old_sbs = zone->superpageblocks;
+	old_kvmalloced = zone->spb_kvmalloced;
+	zone->superpageblocks = new_sbs;
+	zone->nr_superpageblocks = new_nr_sbs;
+	zone->superpageblock_base_pfn = new_sb_base;
+	zone->spb_kvmalloced = true;
+
+	/*
+	 * The boot-time array was allocated with memblock_alloc, which
+	 * is not individually freeable after boot.  Only kvfree arrays
+	 * from previous hotplug resizes.
+	 */
+	if (old_sbs && old_kvmalloced)
+		kvfree(old_sbs);
+}
+#endif /* CONFIG_MEMORY_HOTPLUG */
+
 #ifdef CONFIG_HUGETLB_PAGE_SIZE_VARIABLE
 
 /* Initialise the number of pages represented by NR_PAGEBLOCK_BITS */
--

Signals: acknowledged issue, planned fix
Rik Riel (author) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed a concern about memory fragmentation caused by stealing movable pageblocks for unmovable or reclaimable allocations, and responded by adding an asynchronous evacuation mechanism to migrate remaining movable pages out of the claimed block, allowing future unmovable/reclaimable allocations to be concentrated in fewer pageblocks.
Show original comment 
When the page allocator steals a movable pageblock for unmovable or
reclaimable allocations (via try_to_claim_block), the remaining movable
pages in that block can prevent future unmovable/reclaimable allocations
from being concentrated in fewer pageblocks, leading to long-term memory
fragmentation.

Add a lightweight asynchronous evacuation mechanism: when a movable
pageblock is claimed for unmovable/reclaimable use, queue a work item to
migrate the remaining movable pages out. This allows future
unmovable/reclaimable allocations to be satisfied from the now-evacuated
block, keeping those allocation types concentrated and reducing
fragmentation.

Signed-off-by: Rik van Riel <riel@surriel.com>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4.7 syzkaller

 }
 
+#ifdef CONFIG_COMPACTION
+/*
+ * Pageblock evacuation: asynchronously migrate movable pages out of
+ * pageblocks that were stolen for unmovable/reclaimable allocations.
+ * This keeps unmovable/reclaimable allocations concentrated in fewer
+ * pageblocks, reducing long-term fragmentation.
+ *
+ * Uses a global pool of 64 pre-allocated work items (~3.5KB total)
+ * and a per-pgdat workqueue to keep migration node-local.
+ */
+
+struct evacuate_item {
+	struct work_struct	work;
+	struct zone		*zone;
+	unsigned long		start_pfn;
+	struct llist_node	free_node;
+};
+
+#define NR_EVACUATE_ITEMS	64
+static struct evacuate_item evacuate_pool[NR_EVACUATE_ITEMS];
+static struct llist_head evacuate_freelist;
+
+static struct evacuate_item *evacuate_item_alloc(void)
+{
+	struct llist_node *node;
+
+	node = llist_del_first(&evacuate_freelist);
+	if (!node)
+		return NULL;
+	return container_of(node, struct evacuate_item, free_node);
+}
+
+static void evacuate_item_free(struct evacuate_item *item)
+{
+	llist_add(&item->free_node, &evacuate_freelist);
+}
+
+static void evacuate_pageblock(struct zone *zone, unsigned long start_pfn)
+{
+	unsigned long end_pfn = start_pfn + pageblock_nr_pages;
+	unsigned long pfn = start_pfn;
+	int nr_reclaimed;
+	int ret = 0;
+	struct compact_control cc = {
+		.nr_migratepages = 0,
+		.order = -1,
+		.zone = zone,
+		.mode = MIGRATE_ASYNC,
+		.gfp_mask = GFP_HIGHUSER_MOVABLE,
+	};
+	struct migration_target_control mtc = {
+		.nid = zone_to_nid(zone),
+		.gfp_mask = GFP_HIGHUSER_MOVABLE,
+	};
+
+	/* Verify this pageblock is still worth evacuating */
+	if (get_pageblock_migratetype(pfn_to_page(start_pfn)) == MIGRATE_MOVABLE)
+		return;
+
+	INIT_LIST_HEAD(&cc.migratepages);
+
+	/*
+	 * Loop through the entire pageblock, isolating and migrating
+	 * in batches. isolate_migratepages_range stops at
+	 * COMPACT_CLUSTER_MAX, so we must loop to cover the full block.
+	 */
+	while (pfn < end_pfn || !list_empty(&cc.migratepages)) {
+		if (list_empty(&cc.migratepages)) {
+			cc.nr_migratepages = 0;
+			cc.migrate_pfn = pfn;
+			ret = isolate_migratepages_range(&cc, pfn, end_pfn);
+			if (ret && ret != -EAGAIN)
+				break;
+			pfn = cc.migrate_pfn;
+			if (list_empty(&cc.migratepages))
+				break;
+		}
+
+		nr_reclaimed = reclaim_clean_pages_from_list(zone,
+							&cc.migratepages);
+		cc.nr_migratepages -= nr_reclaimed;
+
+		if (!list_empty(&cc.migratepages)) {
+			ret = migrate_pages(&cc.migratepages,
+					    alloc_migration_target, NULL,
+					    (unsigned long)&mtc, cc.mode,
+					    MR_COMPACTION, NULL);
+			if (ret) {
+				putback_movable_pages(&cc.migratepages);
+				break;
+			}
+		}
+
+		cond_resched();
+	}
+
+	if (!list_empty(&cc.migratepages))
+		putback_movable_pages(&cc.migratepages);
+}
+
+static void evacuate_work_fn(struct work_struct *work)
+{
+	struct evacuate_item *item = container_of(work, struct evacuate_item,
+						  work);
+	evacuate_pageblock(item->zone, item->start_pfn);
+	evacuate_item_free(item);
+}
+
+/**
+ * evacuate_irq_work_fn - IRQ work callback to drain pending evacuations
+ * @work: the irq_work embedded in pg_data_t
+ *
+ * queue_work() can deadlock when called from inside the page allocator
+ * because it may try to allocate memory with locks already held.
+ * Use irq_work to defer the queue_work() calls to a safe context.
+ */
+static void evacuate_irq_work_fn(struct irq_work *work)
+{
+	pg_data_t *pgdat = container_of(work, pg_data_t,
+					evacuate_irq_work);
+	struct llist_node *pending;
+	struct evacuate_item *item, *next;
+
+	if (!pgdat->evacuate_wq)
+		return;
+
+	/*
+	 * Collect all pending items first, then queue them.  Use _safe
+	 * because evacuate_work_fn() may run immediately on another
+	 * CPU and free the item before we follow the next pointer.
+	 */
+	pending = llist_del_all(&pgdat->evacuate_pending);
+	llist_for_each_entry_safe(item, next, pending, free_node) {
+		INIT_WORK(&item->work, evacuate_work_fn);
+		queue_work(pgdat->evacuate_wq, &item->work);
+	}
+}
+
+/**
+ * queue_pageblock_evacuate - schedule async evacuation of movable pages
+ * @zone: the zone containing the pageblock
+ * @pfn: start PFN of the pageblock (must be pageblock-aligned)
+ *
+ * Called from the page allocator when a movable pageblock is claimed
+ * for unmovable or reclaimable allocations. Queues the pageblock for
+ * background migration of its remaining movable pages. Uses irq_work
+ * to defer the actual queue_work() call outside the allocator's lock
+ * context.
+ */
+static void queue_pageblock_evacuate(struct zone *zone, unsigned long pfn)
+{
+	struct evacuate_item *item;
+	pg_data_t *pgdat = zone->zone_pgdat;
+
+	if (!pgdat->evacuate_irq_work.func)
+		return;
+
+	item = evacuate_item_alloc();
+	if (!item)
+		return;
+
+	item->zone = zone;
+	item->start_pfn = pfn;
+	llist_add(&item->free_node, &pgdat->evacuate_pending);
+	irq_work_queue(&pgdat->evacuate_irq_work);
+}
+
+static int __init pageblock_evacuate_init(void)
+{
+	int nid, i;
+
+	/* Initialize the global freelist of work items */
+	init_llist_head(&evacuate_freelist);
+	for (i = 0; i < NR_EVACUATE_ITEMS; i++)
+		llist_add(&evacuate_pool[i].free_node, &evacuate_freelist);
+
+	/* Create a per-pgdat workqueue */
+	for_each_online_node(nid) {
+		pg_data_t *pgdat = NODE_DATA(nid);
+		char name[32];
+
+		snprintf(name, sizeof(name), "kevacuate/%d", nid);
+		pgdat->evacuate_wq = alloc_workqueue(name, WQ_MEM_RECLAIM, 1);
+		if (!pgdat->evacuate_wq) {
+			pr_warn("Failed to create evacuate workqueue for node %d\n", nid);
+			continue;
+		}
+
+		init_llist_head(&pgdat->evacuate_pending);
+		init_irq_work(&pgdat->evacuate_irq_work,

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix is needed
Rik Riel (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about accurately tracking page contents in superpageblocks by extending the pageblock_data flags to include PB_has_unmovable, PB_has_reclaimable, and PB_has_movable bits. The new flags are set at allocation time and cleared on full pageblock free or when migration confirms no pages of that type remain. This change provides a foundation for superpageblock-level steering decisions.
Show original comment 
Extend pageblock_data flags with PB_has_unmovable, PB_has_reclaimable, and
PB_has_movable bits to track the actual types of pages allocated within a
pageblock, independent of its intended migratetype.

The flags are set at steal time in try_to_claim_block(), avoiding overhead
on every allocation in __rmqueue_smallest():

1. Allocation / steal time: when try_to_claim_block() claims a pageblock,
set the PB_has_* flag corresponding to the allocation's migratetype. If
unmovable or reclaimable pages are being placed into a pageblock that
already has PB_has_movable set, queue async evacuation of the remaining
movable pages.

2. Full pageblock free: when buddy merging reconstructs a complete
pageblock in __free_one_page(), clear all PB_has_* flags since the block is
now empty.

3. Migration scan: when isolate_migratepages_block() completes a full
pageblock scan and finds no movable pages to isolate, clear PB_has_movable.
This consolidates the clearing for all callers: evacuate_pageblock(),
compaction, and alloc_contig_range().

This provides the foundation for superpageblock-level steering decisions:
knowing which pageblocks actually contain unmovable/reclaimable pages
allows directing future allocations to already-tainted regions, keeping
clean regions available for large contiguous allocations.

Signed-off-by: Rik van Riel <riel@surriel.com>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4.7 syzkaller

2.54.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Rik Riel (author) Needs Work LLM
The author addressed a concern about lock inversion in the PCP free path, acknowledged that using spin_trylock and falling back to free_one_page() defeats the per-CPU pageset benefits, and proposed a solution involving a per-CPU remote free list (llist) consumed by the owning CPU. The author agreed to implement this change.
Show original comment 
The page allocator's PCP free path needs lock-inversion protection
against zone->lock.  The natural form -- always take pcp->lock with
spin_lock -- can deadlock because callers may hold locks (e.g.
xa_lock via slab/stack_depot) that are also taken in hardirq context,
and pcp->lock is acquired with IRQs enabled on the allocation side.

A coarse fix is to use spin_trylock and fall back to free_one_page()
(direct zone-buddy free) on contention.  That removes the inversion
risk but defeats the per-CPU pageset benefits on a busy multi-CPU
system: many frees take the slow zone->lock path, and the per-CPU
pcp->count visible to allocators understates real free-page
availability for the remote CPU's pageset.

Replace the trylock-fallback with a per-CPU remote free list (llist)
consumed by the owning CPU.  Local frees still use the trylock path;
remote frees push onto the target's lockless llist; the owning CPU
absorbs the queued pages back onto its PCP buddy lists at the next
opportunity.  Result: zero lock-inversion risk, no zone->lock
fallback storm, and remote frees become near-free at the freer's
side.

Mechanics:

  - per_cpu_pages gains struct llist_head free_llist.
  - absorb_remote_frees(pcp) drains the llist into the local PCP buddy
    lists. Called from pcp_rmqueue_smallest(), free_pcppages_bulk(),
    and drain_pages_zone().
  - __free_frozen_pages and free_unref_folios are split into a local
    path (spin_trylock on pcp->lock; on success enqueue locally) and
    a remote path (llist_add to the target CPU's free_llist).
  - The local-side spin_trylock no longer takes irqsave: lockdep
    analysis showed no IRQ-context caller of the local PCP free path
    that is also a holder of pcp->lock; the remote-from-IRQ case
    routes through llist_add (NMI-safe).
  - Memory hot-add lazy init: page_alloc_cpu_dead drains the dead PCP
    via existing drain_pages_zone (which now also drains the llist
    via absorb_remote_frees). For the narrow race where a remote freer
    raced PCPF_CPU_DEAD and pushed onto the dead PCP's llist after the
    drain, page_alloc_cpu_online absorbs any stranded pages.
  - page_alloc_cpu_dead detaches every entry from owned_blocks via
    list_del_init before reinitializing the list head.  A simpler
    INIT_LIST_HEAD-only form leaves owned PB entries with stale
    ->prev/->next pointing at the dead head -- they get list_del()'d
    later by clear_pcpblock_owner() under zone->lock, corrupting
    whatever now happens to be at the dead head address.  A
    stress-test reproducer surfaced this as a list_del prev->next ==
    prev WARN.

QEMU stress (234K worker iters + 5 hotplug cycles + 30 hugepages):
zero WARN/BUG.  Bare-metal test machine ran for ~14 hours under
production-style load with no list_del corruption, no WARN, no panic.

Signed-off-by: Rik van Riel <riel@surriel.com>
Assisted-by: Claude:claude-opus-4.7 syzkaller
